<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="unit-3-research-in-nursing">UNIT 3: RESEARCH IN NURSING<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#unit-3-research-in-nursing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>In this unit you will cover the basic concepts of research, which will include definition, purpose, and contribution of research in the nursing profession. You will also look at the research process, which includes the identification of a researchable topic and developing appropriate research objectives, hypothesis and questions as well as a review of literature that is relevant to your research topic and the research methodology. It also includes data collection, analysis and writing the research report. You will also look at how to develop and implement a research proposal.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="this-unit-is-composed-of-four-sections-and-an-assignment">This unit is composed of four sections and an assignment:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#this-unit-is-composed-of-four-sections-and-an-assignment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Section One: Concepts and Contribution of Research.</p>
<p>Section Two: Types of Research.</p>
<p>Section Three: The Research Process.</p>
<p>Section Four: Developing a Proposal.</p>
<p>Assignment: Conducting Research (Field Work).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="introduction">Introduction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Research is an essential part of the nursing profession. This is because you will not only use it in the planning, implementation and evaluation of your patient's care, but also in the organisation and improvement of the entire health care service countrywide. After covering this unit you will be in a better position to appreciate the significance of research in nursing.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="unit-objectives">Unit Objectives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#unit-objectives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>By the end of this unit you will be able to:</p>
<ul>
<li>Explain the role of research to nursing practice</li>
<li>Describe the major research types</li>
<li>Describe the research process</li>
<li>Develop a research proposal</li>
<li>Implement the research proposal</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="section-1-concepts-and-contribution-of-research">SECTION 1: CONCEPTS AND CONTRIBUTION OF RESEARCH<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#section-1-concepts-and-contribution-of-research" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="introduction">Introduction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In this section you will acquire the basic concepts of research in general, and with specific reference to the nursing profession. The section will cover the definition of research, nursing research, why nurses need to do research and the significance of research in the nursing profession. You will learn how to develop a research topic. It will also cover the ethical and legal issues to consider when carrying out research.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="objectives">Objectives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#objectives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>By the end of this section you will be able to:</p>
<ul>
<li>Define the terms 'research' and 'nursing research'.</li>
<li>Identify the scope of research in nursing.</li>
<li>Explain the significance of research to the nursing profession.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="definition-of-research">Definition of Research<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#definition-of-research" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There are several ways to define the word 'research'. For instance, Treece and Treece (1982, cited in Cormack) define research in its broadest sense as an attempt to gain solutions to problems. More precisely, it is the collection of data in a rigorously controlled situation for the purpose of prediction or explanation. Some writers argue that this definition is not adequate since not all researchers will be finding solutions to problems. Neither does all research need to be carried out in a rigorously controlled situation such as in a laboratory. This is just an indication for you to appreciate how difficult it is to get one single definition of the word 'research'.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="scientific-definition-of-research">Scientific Definition of Research<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#scientific-definition-of-research" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>According to Burns and Grove (1999: 3), the word 'research' means 'to search again' or 'to examine carefully'. More specifically, research is diligent, systematic inquiry or study to validate and refine existing knowledge and develop new knowledge. This definition indicates some of the functions of research. It further explains the word 'research' in its literal use and its scientific use at the same time. Take for instance, the terms 'search again' or 'examine carefully'. This means the researcher (the person carrying out the research) has to search for the facts over</p>
<p>and over again. They have to re-examine all facts at hand carefully.</p>
<p>Similarly as Burns and Grove (1999) indicate 'diligence' and 'systematic study' will include certain activities, such as planning, organisation and persistence. Research is meant to validate, that is, prove or disprove existing knowledge or information. For instance, if a certain nursing practice is to be evaluated, then a researcher will need to investigate it and see whether the specific practice is still relevant or needs modification. It is also important to note that in research 'the systematic study' must use the scientific process.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="definition-of-nursing-research">Definition of Nursing Research<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#definition-of-nursing-research" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Having covered and defined the term 'research', it is now important for you to define 'nursing research'. As with the definition of research, there is no single definition for nursing research, either. However, for the purposes of this unit, you will use the following definition by Cormark (1991:5), which states that nursing research is defined as research into those aspects of professional activity, which are predominantly and appropriately the concern and responsibility of nurses.</p>
<p>As indicated here, research can be nursing research only when it addresses issues that are relevant to the nursing profession. If the topic is not going to have an impact on the nursing practice, it is not nursing research. Thus, it is important to understand that in order to define your research as nursing research; your study must be geared towards improving certain aspects of the nursing practice, education and administration.</p>
<p>However, this does not mean that nurses cannot venture into other non-nursing research. Nurses can undertake any type of research but if they would like to carry out nursing research, then the study should be relevant to the nursing profession.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nature-and-scope-of-research-in-nursing">Nature and Scope of Research in Nursing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nature-and-scope-of-research-in-nursing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Now that you have identified a general definition of research and a specific definition of nursing research, your next step is to find out what nursing research entails and whether nurses really need to do research.</p>
<p>In module one, you covered professionalism in nursing. You learnt that for a profession to be recognised, it must have its own knowledge base. You might have also learnt that a large</p>
<p>portion of knowledge in nursing is either borrowed from other professions or is based on traditions, beliefs and instincts that, for the most part, have not been validated by empirical studies.</p>
<p>In order for the nursing profession to have its own body of knowledge, its professional practice must be based on a sound knowledge developed through scientific research. The knowledge base should come from the various aspects of nursing education, nursing management, nursing practice or from general nursing experience.</p>
<p>Nursing research is not an old subject even though nurses have made tremendous achievements in the area in the last few decades.</p>
<p>Initially, nursing research in the early part of the 20th century concentrated on nursing education and the role of nursing and nurses. However, as nurses became aware and acquainted themselves with the importance of research, there has been an increased number of studies targeted at clinical nursing problems (Burns &amp; Grove, 1999).</p>
<p>This started in the 1970s and to-date clinical oriented research problems continue to be the main focus of research for nurses. The importance of nurses' involvement in research is that it enhances the development of the knowledge base of the nursing profession. Research findings aim to develop and refine scientific knowledge for use in practice (Burns &amp; Grove, 1999). Thus, while nursing research is mainly concerned with investigating areas of significance to the nursing profession, it is nonetheless, limited to nursing and nurses although they are at liberty to engage in research on non-nursing areas.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="significance-and-contribution-of-nursing-research">Significance and Contribution of Nursing Research<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#significance-and-contribution-of-nursing-research" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You have already learnt some of the benefits of nursing research in the previous sections. A brief description of the purposes of nursing research has been outlined by Cormack (1992:7) among others.</p>
<p>The purposes of nursing research include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Developing scientific, evidence based reasons for nursing activities.</li>
<li>Finding ways of increasing the cost-effectiveness of nursing activities.</li>
<li>Providing a basis for standards setting and quality assurance.</li>
</ul>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<ul>
<li>Providing evidence in support of demands for resources in nursing.</li>
<li>Barring and defending a professional status for nursing.</li>
<li>Focusing on priority problems that affect the nursing profession.</li>
</ul>
<p>The points in the previous page elaborate the purposes of nursing research, but lead you to ask why research is such an important component of the nursing profession. In answering this question, you can look at some suggestions offered by some nursing researchers. A good example is Burns &amp; Grove (1999), who state that nursing research helps in:</p>
<ul>
<li>Describing certain nursing observations (phenomenon).</li>
<li>Explaining certain nursing practices, for instance, the relationship between specific variables (characteristics or attributes).</li>
<li>Predicting the outcome of nursing activities undertaken by nurses and other health workers.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="section-2-types-of-research-introduction">SECTION 2: TYPES OF RESEARCH INTRODUCTION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#section-2-types-of-research-introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>When describing types of research, researchers use several methods of classification. According to UNISA (2000: 83), research can be classified as follows:</p>
<ul>
<li>Categories, that is, what the study is to be used for.</li>
<li>Methodological approaches, which includes the research approaches that</li>
</ul>
<p>the researcher intends to adapt in the course of the study.</p>
<ul>
<li>Aims of the study, including what the study aims at achieving.</li>
<li>The time dimension referring to the time factor of the study, that is, is the study a 'one off' case or one that will take months or even years.</li>
</ul>
<p>In broad terms, research can be classified into qualitative and quantitative research approaches.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="objectives">Objectives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#objectives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>By the end of this section you will be able to:</p>
<ul>
<li>Describe the qualitative research approach</li>
<li>Describe the quantitative research approach</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="qualitative-methods">Qualitative Methods<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#qualitative-methods" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>According to Burns and Grove (1999:338), qualitative research is 'a systematic, subjective approach used to describe life experiences and give them meaning'. It focuses on the whole issue under investigation, which is in line with the holistic approach the nursing profession advocates for in patient care. As you will see in the table below, there are major distinguishing features between qualitative and quantitative research types. Qualitative research is a type of research where the data collected is in the form of words rather than numbers. The data collected from this type of research is then grouped into categories, themes and patterns (Maalim, 1999).</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Distinguishing Features of Quantitative and Qualitative Research (Adopted from Brink, H.I. (1996:13)</th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Quantitative</td><td>Qualitative</td></tr><tr><td>Focuses on a relatively small number of concepts (concise and narrow).</td><td>Attempts to understand the phenomenon in its entirety.</td></tr><tr><td>Begins with preconceived ideas about how the concepts are interrelated.</td><td>Has few preconceived ideas and stresses the importance of people's interpretations of events and circumstances, rather than the researcher's interpretations.</td></tr><tr><td>Uses structured procedures and formal instruments to collect information.</td><td>Collects information mainly without formal structured instruments.</td></tr><tr><td>Collects information under controlled conditions.</td><td>Does not attempt to control the context of the research, but rather attempts to capture that context in its entirety.</td></tr><tr><td>Emphasises objectivity in the collection and analysis of information.</td><td>Assumes that subjectivity is essential for understanding of human experience.</td></tr><tr><td>Analyses numeric information through statistical procedures.</td><td>Analyses narrative information in an organised, but intuitive fashion.</td></tr><tr><td>Investigator does not participate in the events</td><td>Involves sustained interaction with the people being</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 79 of 185
Bishop's Computers</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>under investigation and is most likely to collect data from a real distance.</th><th>studied in their own language, and on their own turf.</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Incorporates logistic, deductive reasoning.</td><td>Inductive and dialectic reasoning are predominant.</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The data from qualitative research is usually collected through:</p>
<ul>
<li>Observation, that is, a participant observer or a non participant observer.</li>
<li>Focus group discussions.</li>
<li>Face to face interviews.</li>
<li>Posted or self administered questionnaires.</li>
</ul>
<p>Qualitative research can further be sub divided into several categories including historical, case studies, observations, and rapid rural appraisal (which is a more recent innovative approach used in participatory research).</p>
<p>You will now look at each of these categories in more detail.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="historical-research">Historical Research<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#historical-research" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is a type of qualitative research that attempts to describe and learn from the past. This type of research is mainly used by social scientists. Historical research is a descriptive type of research where the investigator attempts to record and describe, in a sequential manner, the events under investigation. The findings are then presented in the fullest possible picture. A good example is where a nurse may investigate the historical development of nursing in Kenya and show the events and trends associated with the profession in Kenya.</p>
<p>Historical research has the advantage of teaching us about the past so as to put the problems of today into their proper context. However, when doing historical research, it is important that the researcher plans properly, laying out clear objectives to ensure that they are not overwhelmed by the large amount of materials, losing track of the study. In the recent past, many nurse researchers have ventured into this type of research and produced important results.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="case-study">Case Study<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#case-study" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Another type of a qualitative research method is the case study. A case study is defined as 'an in-depth investigation of an individual, group, institution or phenomena' (Mugenda &amp; Mugenda, 1999: 173). According to Moore (1988) case studies are used when narrowing the research focus into fewer units or increasing the range of units within the study.</p>
<p>Case studies are commonly used when the investigator is attempting to understand complex phenomena, or the various causes and effects of a certain phenomenon. This is one of the most common research methods used by nurses. Case studies have the advantage of requiring minimal resources when compared with other types of research. Case studies also provide a means of looking in depth at complex situations. At the same time, there are certain limitations associated with case studies, which researchers need to be aware of. These include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Results from case studies cannot be statistically validated because no sampling strategy is involved.</li>
<li>Case studies can be time consuming, particularly when looking into complex long term issues or phenomena.</li>
<li>Case studies are more susceptible to the 'Hawthorne effect', where the research process influences the study outcome.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="rapid-rural-appraisal-rra">Rapid Rural Appraisal (RRA)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#rapid-rural-appraisal-rra" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A third type of qualitative research is referred to as Rapid Rural Appraisal (RRA). This is an innovative research method used for collecting data in participatory research. This approach emphasises the need for the researcher to acknowledge and appreciate that local research respondents have the knowledge and skills to be partners in the research process (Maalim, 1999). In RRA, both the researcher and the respondents collaborate and learn from each other, getting possible solutions for the research questions (Rifkin, 1996). RRA is important for generating data from the local community such as disease patterns which they (community members) are quite familiar with. There are many RRA tools and techniques for data collection but the following are some of the more common ones.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="mapping">Mapping<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mapping" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is drawing of sketch maps by the research</p>
<p>respondents to identify the comparative location and importance of different resources within the research area. This may include health facilities, social amenities and infrastructure like roads, transport and means of communication (Maalim, 1999).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="daily-schedules">Daily Schedules<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#daily-schedules" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This involves outlining the daily activities of community members. Daily schedules are used to identify daily labour patterns and other activities (Jones, 1996:2). This tool enables the researcher to identify and discuss the daily workloads of the community members according to individuals, gender and seasons.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="venn-diagrams">Venn Diagrams<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#venn-diagrams" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is the use of circles to indicate the relationships, importance and interactions between individuals and institutions within the research areas. Usually the respondents draw circles of varying sizes. The size of the circle and the distance from the centre indicate the importance of the institution, for example a health centre, to the respondent.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="seasonal-calendars">Seasonal Calendars<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#seasonal-calendars" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>As the name suggests, this tool is used for gaining information from the research respondents concerning seasonal variations in patterns concerning certain issues of interest. This tool could, for example, enable the researcher to gather data on disease patterns according to the seasons.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="quantitative-methods">Quantitative Methods<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#quantitative-methods" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Having covered qualitative research as one of the major research types, you will now look at quantitative research. Burns &amp; Grove (1999:23) defined quantitative research as a 'formal, objective, rigorous, systematic process for generating information about the world', with respect to populations' dynamics, characteristics and phenomenon.</p>
<p>It is important to note that, for many years, the quantitative research method has been the preferred method by nurse researchers. This was primarily because, in the early days, quantitative research was seen as the only recognised research or, as it was then called, 'the scientific method'. However, this no longer is the case as researchers have now</p>
<p>understood that qualitative research is as important as, and complementary to, quantitative research methods. Researchers now appreciate that the selection of either quantitative or qualitative research types depends on many factors, including:</p>
<ul>
<li>The issue (phenomenon) under investigation.</li>
<li>The aims and objectives of the research.</li>
<li>The state of existing knowledge.</li>
</ul>
<p>At the same time, many researchers now acknowledge that 'although qualitative research and quantitative research do differ, they can also, in specific areas, complement each other' (Grobbelaar 2000: 91).</p>
<p>Quantitative research is also subdivided into several types, which you will now cover in detail.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="descriptive-research">Descriptive Research<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#descriptive-research" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Descriptive research is 'the exploration and description of a phenomenon in real-life situations' (Burns &amp; Grove 1999: 24). It involves the systematic collection and presentation of data to give a clear picture of a particular situation. A good example of descriptive study is a survey. Descriptive research also involves the systematic collection of information and aims to explore and describe new facts about a situation, people, activities or events. Descriptive research is important in nursing for the following reasons:</p>
<ul>
<li>Assists in the development of new knowledge.</li>
<li>Forms the basis for future research.</li>
<li>Generates questions and hypotheses for further experimental study.</li>
</ul>
<p>In descriptive research, the main focus is the investigation of existing practices, beliefs and attitudes held by people or investigating current trends of nursing</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="experimental-research">Experimental Research<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#experimental-research" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is one of the most complex and important research types. It is also commonly used in clinical settings because of its accuracy and reliability. Experimental research has, for several decades, been referred to as the true or scientific research because of its strict adherence to the three major characteristics of control, manipulation and randomisation.</p>
<p>A major difference between experimental and</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>non experimental research types that you as a research student need to note is that, in experimental research, the researcher is the active agent rather than remaining a passive observer (Polit and Hungler, 1999). According to Burnard and Morrison (1990: 30), the major distinction between experimental and descriptive research types include the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>Descriptive research merely describes something, whereas experimental research tests a hypothesis.</li>
<li>Researchers can control variables in experimental research but cannot do this with descriptive research.</li>
<li>Descriptive research may not have control of the study subjects but experimental research has full control of the subjects under study.</li>
<li>Experimental research can lead to predictions but descriptive research may not.</li>
</ul>
<p>Many times, experimental research is subdivided into 'true experimental' and quasi-experimental. The true experimental definition refers to the method you have just covered. You will now look at quasi-experimental research.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="quasi-experimental-research">Quasi-experimental Research<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#quasi-experimental-research" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is a type of quantitative research whose purpose is to examine causal relationships or to determine the effects of one variable on another (Burns &amp; Grove, 1999). Quasi-experimental studies involve implementing a specific treatment and then later examining the effects of this treatment using selected methods of measurement. Quasi-experimental study is a type of intervention study.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260117-1bf29b2b.jpeg" alt="img-0.jpeg"></p>
<p>The purpose of quasi-experimental study is to examine causality. Quasi-experimental designs are developed in order to provide alternative</p>
<p>means of examining causality in situations not conducive to experimental control.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="classification-of-research">Classification of Research<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#classification-of-research" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Research can be classified based on duration of study. There are two main types of research studies, these are longitudinal and cross sectional studies.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="longitudinal-studies">Longitudinal Studies<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#longitudinal-studies" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>These are the types of studies that are conducted over a long duration of time. A good example is the cohort study where individuals having a characteristic of interest (exposure) are followed over a duration of time. During the time of study the subjects are observed to see if they develop an outcome of interest.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="cross-sectional-studies">Cross Sectional Studies<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#cross-sectional-studies" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>These are the studies that are conducted over a short duration of time, for example, a few weeks. Unlike the longitudinal studies they tend to be relatively cheaper. They are initiated where results are required as a matter of urgency.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="section-3-the-research-process">SECTION 3: THE RESEARCH PROCESS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#section-3-the-research-process" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="introduction">Introduction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In this section, you will learn about the 'research process'; a very important and significant section of research, and indeed the kingpin of the whole research topic. Included in this section, is a study of 'the steps' involved in the research process.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="objectives">Objectives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#objectives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>By the end of this section you will be able to:</p>
<ul>
<li>Identify a research topic</li>
<li>Formulate a research problem</li>
<li>Formulate research objectives</li>
<li>Describe the justification for your research</li>
<li>Explain how to conduct literature review</li>
<li>Describe the research methodology</li>
<li>Explain the methods of measurement</li>
<li>Design the data collection instruments</li>
<li>Explain the implementation of the data collection tools</li>
</ul>
<p>Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers</p>
<ul>
<li>Describe the process of analysing the data collected</li>
<li>Write a scientific research report</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-research-process">The Research Process<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-research-process" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It is important to appreciate that the various steps of the research process are not delineated from each other as such, but indeed cyclical in nature, whereby one step leads to the other for its effectiveness. Likewise, it is important to note that researchers have greatly differed in their estimate of the number of steps the research process should consist of. Most researchers have given steps ranging in number from between ten to sixteen.</p>
<p>Despite the large difference in the number of steps that should constitute the research process, the majority of researchers do agree on four general phases of the research process. Brink (1996:56) listed the following four phases as being key to the research process</p>
<p>â˜ The conceptual phase, also called the thinking or planning phase.
â˜ The empirical phase, also called the doing phase.
â˜ The interpretive phase, or the phase where the researcher looks at the meaning of it all.
â˜ The communication phase or the phase of writing and disseminating the research report.</p>
<p>You will now further sub-divide these phases into ten steps for the purpose of your study and look at each step in detail.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="step-one-identification-of-a-research-topic">Step One: Identification of a Research Topic<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#step-one-identification-of-a-research-topic" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Having identified some of the characteristics of good nursing research, you need also to learn how to identify a researchable topic. For a researcher to identify a good research topic they will require first to list down some of the topics or problems that need to be investigated. It is important to put this in order of priority and then analyse each topic or problem individually.</p>
<p>A research problem is an issue that requires a solution. It could, for instance, involve looking at why certain things are done the way they are</p>
<p>done and not the other way round. In actual fact, a research problem endeavours to answer or address a specific problem or situation. In nursing research the issue that needs to be addressed should be one related to the nursing profession.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260117-593d8b70.jpeg" alt="img-1.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-research-process">The Research Process<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-research-process" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You will note that your research starts with the identification of a research problem, which you feel is serious and important enough for you to spend your time researching. This research problem then becomes your research topic and will become the title of your research paper.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-are-some-of-the-criteria-you-should-look-for-when-identifying-a-research-topic">What are some of the criteria you should look for when identifying a research topic?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-are-some-of-the-criteria-you-should-look-for-when-identifying-a-research-topic" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There are certain characteristics or criteria that a good research topic in nursing should possess to ensure that the study is relevant not only to the nursing practice but also to the entire health care delivery system.</p>
<ul>
<li>Improve nursing services by contributing to more knowledge and better skills.</li>
<li>Enhance existing knowledge by filling in gaps that exist.</li>
<li>Encourage more research on already existing nursing theories.</li>
<li>Address current concerns or priority areas in nursing.</li>
</ul>
<p>Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers</p>
<ul>
<li>Pay more attention to ethical issues (this will be discussed in detail later).</li>
</ul>
<p>In addition to this, the researcher undertaking the study should have the necessary knowledge and skills. They should also be accorded the necessary resources such as money, manpower, materials and access to the research subjects.</p>
<p>These characteristics are of great significance to the researcher because when adhered to, they ensure that the study is well defined and hence feasible to undertake. It is important to point out that this list is not exhaustive and, as you progress with your research readings, you will learn to identify other important characteristics that a good research topic should have.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="how-can-you-identify-an-appropriate-research-topic">How can you identify an appropriate research topic?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#how-can-you-identify-an-appropriate-research-topic" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Interestingly, among all researchers there is no way to find a researchable topic without having experienced the problem or read about it. It is obvious that, one way or the other, there must be a need/problem that the researcher has experienced and hence feels the need to seek a solution to. It is also possible that research topics are usually derived from problems, but at times, point the way towards making certain changes or modifications from an existing situation.</p>
<p>According to Collins et al (2000), there are three major possible areas that researchers may draw on for their research topics.</p>
<p>A researcher may draw research topics from:</p>
<ul>
<li>Existing professional knowledge and experience. It is highly possible that in the course of your work, you have faced several questions that you would like to find answers for. This is indeed the most common source of an appropriate research topic.</li>
<li>Socially significant issues, that is, the research topic has practical relevance and significance to the society. Here, you as the researcher have to identify the implications of the study to the society. For instance, the study could be in a position to solve an existing and pressing social problem. You as the nurse in-charge of a health centre might have realised that since the introduction of cost-sharing exercises, not many of the low income members of the community are coming for treatment to the health centre. You may decide to</li>
</ul>
<p>find out why and propose a solution to the problem.</p>
<ul>
<li>The study must have scientific relevance and significance as well. It is important to show that the research will, at the end, have academic and/or scientific relevance.</li>
</ul>
<p>This could be used for improving or modifying patients' care.</p>
<p>Therefore, when identifying a possible research topic, look at some of the above factors or others that you think are important for the relevance of your study.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="step-two-formulation-of-research-objectives-hypothesis-and-questions">Step Two: Formulation of Research Objectives, Hypothesis and Questions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#step-two-formulation-of-research-objectives-hypothesis-and-questions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In this step, you shall learn how to formulate research objectives and research questions. So far you have learnt how to identify a researchable topic. You have also learnt about the different methods of research and their divisions. In this section of the research process you are aiming to 'describe the formulation of research objectives and research questions'.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260117-9041ae20.jpeg" alt="img-2.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-is-a-research-objective">What is a research objective?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-is-a-research-objective" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A research objective is a clear, concise, declarative, statement expressed to direct a study.</p>
<p>It focuses on identification and description of variables and/or determination of the relationships among variables. Research</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>objectives and questions are important for several reasons.</p>
<p>Research objectives help to:</p>
<ul>
<li>Bridge the gap between the research purpose and the study design.</li>
<li>Guide on planning for data collection and analysis.</li>
<li>Summarise what is to be achieved by the study.</li>
<li>Build a close link with the statement of the problem.</li>
<li>Keep the researcher within the scope of study by defining the area of focus.</li>
</ul>
<p>Research objectives are sub-divided into broad and specific objectives. When formulating good research objectives, the objectives should have the following characteristics, using the acronym 'SMART'.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-do-you-understand-by-the-acronym-smart">What do you understand by the acronym 'SMART'?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-do-you-understand-by-the-acronym-smart" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The acronym SMART stands for:
S - Specific
M - Measurable
A - Achievable
R - Realistic
T - Time bound</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="example-of-a-smart-objective">Example of a SMART Objective<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#example-of-a-smart-objective" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>To establish the number of children born at home within the last two years in Ganga village. Specific - Establishing prevalence of home deliveries in Ganga village Measurable - Number of children Achievable - The task is achievable Realistic - The task is realistic Time bound - In the last two years</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="example-of-a-non-smart-objective">Example of a Non SMART Objective<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#example-of-a-non-smart-objective" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>To find out the level of home deliveries.</p>
<p>The objective is neither specific, measurable, achievable nor realistic. It has also not set out the time frame.</p>
<p>This means the research objective should be specific, in that, it clearly identifies the item at hand for investigation. The objective should also be 'measurable' by being quantifiable. A good objective should be 'achievable' and 'realistic' so that the researcher is able to acquire the set objectives on time (time bound) in form of human, financial and material resources.</p>
<p>Once the researcher has formulated appropriate objectives, the next move is to formulate relevant research question(s). It is important to</p>
<p>understand that without a clearly and well-defined research question, it will be difficult to complete the research process. Sometimes inexperienced researchers find it difficult to formulate questions. Questions can be derived from:</p>
<ul>
<li>Your working experience (professional experience).</li>
<li>Literature search you are undertaking.</li>
<li>Theoretical frameworks on nursing.</li>
</ul>
<p>It is important to ensure that the research questions are 'good research questions'. One way of doing this is to use the acronym 'FINER'. The acronym 'FINER' stands for the following:</p>
<p>F - Feasible, allowing one to appreciate the practical limitations.</p>
<p>I - Interesting, sustaining the research process.</p>
<p>N - Novel, able to provide new findings</p>
<p>E - Ethical.</p>
<p>R - Relevant, advancing science or influencing clinical care, health care policy among others.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="example-of-a-finer-research-question">Example of a FINER Research Question<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#example-of-a-finer-research-question" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Do nurses in Kalala hospital practice the hand washing procedure as stipulated by the hospital infection control handbook?</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="example-of-a-non-finer-research-question">Example of a non FINER Research Question<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#example-of-a-non-finer-research-question" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Are nurses washing hands?</p>
<p>In general, the formulation of a research question assists the researcher to:</p>
<ul>
<li>Focus on the study by narrowing it down to the essentials</li>
<li>Avoid collection of data that are not necessary</li>
<li>Organise the study in clearly defined parts or phases</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="hypothesis-of-the-study">Hypothesis of the Study<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hypothesis-of-the-study" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Having defined and understood the research objectives and research questions, the other concept that you may need to develop during your research is the research hypothesis because not all research is tenable to hypothesis testing, hence some research may include hypotheses while others will not.</p>
<p>Most of the descriptive studies do not have a hypothesis to test but they generate hypothesis. Experimental studies have hypothesis to test.</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 85 of 185
Bishop's Computers</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-is-a-hypothesis">What is a hypothesis?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-is-a-hypothesis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Brink (1996:91) defines a hypothesis as â€˜the formulation of the expected relationship between two or more variables in a specific populationâ€™. It is the researcherâ€™s prediction or explanation of the relationships between two variables. For example:</p>
<p>Persons with Type II diabetes mellitus who have greater knowledge of their diseases will have a higher rate of adherence to treatment regimen than those with less knowledge.</p>
<p>In the example given, the predicted relationship is between â€˜knowledgeâ€™ and â€˜adherence to treatment regimenâ€™. Hypotheses are applied in quantitative studies in order to guide the study, for example, quasi-experimental and experimental studies. A hypothesis is usually tested and, subsequently either rejected or fail to be rejected by the researcher. The hypothesis helps the study in suggesting prediction, explaining outcome and in guiding the investigation to provide a focus.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-are-some-of-the-types-of-hypotheses">What are some of the types of hypotheses?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-are-some-of-the-types-of-hypotheses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There are various types of hypotheses.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="directional-hypothesis">Directional Hypothesis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#directional-hypothesis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A directional hypothesis predicts an outcome in a specific direction. One example is the hypothesis given, which stated that:</p>
<p>Persons with Type II diabetes mellitus and have greater knowledge of their diseases will have a higher rate of adherence to treatment regimen than those with less knowledge.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="non-directional-hypothesis">Non Directional Hypothesis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#non-directional-hypothesis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This indicates there is a difference or correlation but does not specify which. For example:</p>
<p>Persons with Type II diabetes mellitus who follow a structured programme on their condition have a higher rate of adherence to treatment.</p>
<p>This does not indicate a directional relationship.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="null-hypothesis-and-alternative-hypothesis">Null Hypothesis and Alternative Hypothesis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#null-hypothesis-and-alternative-hypothesis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Null hypothesis (denoted as <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msub><mi>H</mi><mn>0</mn></msub></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">H_0</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8333em; vertical-align: -0.15em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.08125em;">H</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.3011em;"><span style="top: -2.55em; margin-left: -0.0813em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight">0</span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">â€‹</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.15em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span>)
The null (statistical) hypothesis is used for statistical testing and interpretation. It states no difference exists between groups or no correlations between variables.</li>
<li>Alternative hypothesis (denoted as <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msub><mi>H</mi><mn>1</mn></msub></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">H_1</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8333em; vertical-align: -0.15em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.08125em;">H</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.3011em;"><span style="top: -2.55em; margin-left: -0.0813em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight">1</span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">â€‹</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.15em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span>)
This is the alternate hypothesis. It states that there is a difference or correlation.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="variables">Variables<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#variables" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="what-is-a-variable">What is a variable?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-is-a-variable" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>As you may have observed, when looking at the research questions and research hypothesis, reference was made several times to the term â€˜variablesâ€™. You, therefore, need to define what a variable is. Variables are defined as quality, properties or characteristics of persons, things or situation that change or vary. For example: sex (male and female) age (20â€“25, 26â€“30 years) academic success, stress and pain. There are four main types of variables that you may come across as a research student.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="independent-variable-treatment-or-experimental-variable">Independent Variable (Treatment or Experimental Variable)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#independent-variable-treatment-or-experimental-variable" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>This is a variable that influences other variables. It is perceived as contributing to or enabling a particular outcome. It is the intervention or treatment that the researcher performs to see the resulting change in the dependant variable. It is also referred to as the input.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="dependent-variable">Dependent Variable<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#dependent-variable" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>This is the outcome variable. It reflects the effects (outcome) or response to the independent variable. The dependent variable is the variable that appears, disappears, diminishes or increases. For example, to determine the effects of salt intake on hypertension, the blood pressure is the dependant variable and salt intake is the independent variable.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="extraneous-variables">Extraneous Variables<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#extraneous-variables" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>These are uncontrolled variables that influence the findings of the research study. They influence both the dependent and independent variables. These are called threats to internal and external validity of the study and may bias the selection, the time factor, and the instrument used.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="demographic-variables">Demographic Variables<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#demographic-variables" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>As the name suggests these are demographic attributes. They are variables that cannot be manipulated or influenced by the researcher, for example, age, sex religious beliefs or educational level.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="step-three-rationale-or-justification-of-the-research-problem">Step Three: Rationale or Justification of the Research Problem<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#step-three-rationale-or-justification-of-the-research-problem" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The words 'rationale' and 'justification' are commonly used interchangeably. This is the section of the study that outlines reasons for carrying out the study. Justifications of the study should address some of the following questions:</p>
<ul>
<li>What gaps in knowledge will the study address?</li>
<li>Why is the study important?</li>
<li>What will the study contribute to the society?</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260117-6796b267.jpeg" alt="img-3.jpeg">
The Research Process</p>
<p>Meanwhile, the significance of the study addresses questions like:</p>
<ul>
<li>How will the results be used?</li>
<li>Who will benefit from the results?</li>
<li>Is the study worth it?</li>
</ul>
<p>It is important to state the justification convincingly so as to rationalise the utilisation of resources such as time, money materials and manpower. The rationale of the study should describe the utility and importance of the problem in health care services in general and the nursing profession in particular.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="step-four-literature-review">Step Four: Literature Review<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#step-four-literature-review" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260117-58007493.jpeg" alt="img-4.jpeg">
The Research Process</p>
