<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="review-of-existing-records">Review of Existing Records<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#review-of-existing-records" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="what-are-some-of-the-advantages-of-reviewing-existing-data">What are some of the advantages of reviewing existing data?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-are-some-of-the-advantages-of-reviewing-existing-data" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The review of existing records has several advantages. Data collection in any study entails heavy costs in terms of time and money. By reviewing existing data, you should be able to save time and money (Treece and Treece, 1986). For example, if you were to carry out a large scale field survey to determine the national immunisation coverage in Kenya. You would not only require an immense amount of funds but also adequate time to undertake the survey. By reviewing existing data, you the researcher, saves both time and money.</p>
<p>Every researcher endeavours to limit respondent bias. Existing data utilises records, which are unbiased, as the person who collected the data had no knowledge of the future use to which it would be put. Additionally, existing records cover a long period of time, which is particularly useful to the researcher who can only dedicate a short time to their research.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260117-e8025bf8.jpeg" alt="img-9.jpeg"></p>
<p>The Research Process</p>
<p>Existing records also save the researcher from the worries and concerns of seeking the</p>
<p>cooperation of the respondents. When you set out to collect data from a respondent for a study, you have to obtain informed consent from the respondent or guardian, where the respondent is a minor or mentally incapable of making decisions. When reviewing existing records, all that you require is the authority to access relevant records, since the data has already been collected.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-are-the-disadvantages-of-existing-records-or-data">What are the disadvantages of existing records or data?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-are-the-disadvantages-of-existing-records-or-data" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Pre-existing data also has its share of shortcomings. Most notably, since the researcher is not responsible for the collection and recording of the data, limiting biases and even ascertaining the authenticity of the data would</p>
<p>be difficult. This can often result in doubts about the validity of the data.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="structured-interview-schedule">Structured Interview Schedule<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#structured-interview-schedule" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A structured interview schedule is a formal and written document where questions are asked orally in either face-to-face or telephone interviews.</p>
<p>The responses are then recorded by the researcher (Bogdan and Biklen, 1992; Oppenheim, 1993). The main advantage of a highly structured questionnaire is that it provides comparability of responses and facilitates analysis (Polit and Hungler, 1993). Highly structured instruments are quite common in quantitative research methods where the application of statistical tests is used to predict the reliability and validity of the data.</p>
<p>The interview schedule is one of the structured instruments used by researchers for collecting data. It is commonly used in telephone interviews but is also applicable to a special category of respondents, such as children, the elderly and the illiterate who may not be able to read and write (Oppenheim, 1993). The instrument is expected to have a high response rate, since the researcher administers it personally. An interview schedule also has the advantage of capturing the respondent's own words.</p>
<p>Despite the aforementioned advantages, the instrument has several limitations that the researcher should be aware of. The instrument demands a much longer time to complete than other instruments, such as questionnaires. In addition, due to the presence of the researcher, respondents may withhold certain vital</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>information or even change information to please the researcher.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="focus-group-discussions">Focus Group Discussions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#focus-group-discussions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>According to Polit and Hungler, (1993:202) 'Focus group interviews are interviews with groups of 5-15 people whose opinions and experiences are solicited simultaneously. The advantage of a focus group discussion is that it is efficient and can generate dialogue. Focus group discussions have the ability to generate information from the group members. The composition of the group is usually limited to those with similar characteristics, such as socio economic status, so that the members feel free in contributing to the issue at hand. The instrument allows the members to share their views, experiences and opinions. The interpersonal interactions create a free and enjoyable environment.</p>
<p>Krueger (1994:6) notes that 'the discussion is comfortable and often enjoyable for the participants as they share their ideas and perceptions'. Group members influence each other by responding to the ideas and comments in the discussion.</p>
<p>Several advantages accrue from such an instrument. The instrument allows a large number of respondents to be interviewed at one go, which saves time and money. However, one major shortcoming is that the method, because of the number of respondents involved, calls for diligence and skill in ensuring that the process runs smoothly and yields the desired information.</p>
<p>Novice researchers often find this very difficult as the researcher is expected to exercise great care in ensuring that the discussion remains within context while at the same time limiting disruption of the discussion process.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="in-depth-interviews">In-depth Interviews<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#in-depth-interviews" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The researcher can also utilise face-to-face in-depth interviews using semi-structured questionnaires for key informants. Key informant interviews are defined as interviews with people who have special positions in the community and whose opinions and experiences are seen as representative of a whole group. They often see the problems of a group rather than of an individual, making them a very useful group of people to interview.</p>
<p>Semi-structured questionnaires possess a flexibility that allows the researcher to gather in depth information. The instrument enables the participants to give responses in a narrative form and is quite useful in qualitative research (Polit and Hungler, 1993). The respondent takes the lead and determines the flow of the conversation, which is of great importance when new areas are to be investigated (Polit and Hungler, 1993). Key informant interviews provide valuable and independent information about the research population within a short span of time and save time and money. However, Polit and Hungler (1993) suggest that the main weakness of the instrument is that it requires the researcher to be very articulate.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="non-participatory-structured-observation">Non Participatory Structured Observation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#non-participatory-structured-observation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Another instrument that can be used for data collection is non participatory, semi-structured observation. Non participatory observation is observing a given situation from the outside. The observer declares their intention to observe and goes ahead to watch the activities being carried out without asking questions or interfering in any way. As the activities to be observed progress, the observer remains in the background, keenly observing and noting down events without comment.</p>
<p>Polit and Hungler (1993: 220), with reference to the relevance of the observational method as a tool for generating data, state that: 'Observational methods have an intrinsic appeal with respect to their ability to capture a record of behaviours and events.'</p>
<p>Furthermore, there is virtually no other data collection method that can provide the depth and variety of information as observation. With this approach, the observers are used as measuring instruments and provide a uniquely sensitive and intelligent tool.</p>
<p>Once you have generated the data, it is important to follow a structured method of data processing and entry. The following steps, adapted from Floyd and Fowler (1993:123), provide a useful guideline:</p>
<ul>
<li>Decide on a format, that is, the way the data will be organised in a file.</li>
<li>Design the code, that is, identify the rules by which respondent's answers will be assigned values that can be processed by a computer.</li>
<li>Code the data. This refers to the process of turning responses into standard categories.</li>
<li>Input data into the computer if you are going to use analytic software.</li>
</ul>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 98 of 185
Bishop's Computers</p>
<ul>
<li>Completeness and consistency before analysis.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="step-seven-conducting-a-pre-test-or-pilot-study">Step Seven: Conducting a Pre-Test or Pilot Study<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#step-seven-conducting-a-pre-test-or-pilot-study" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Before you carry out the full scale data collection process, it is important to conduct a trial to see whether the instruments that you will use for data collection are actually appropriate for the study and whether you are collecting the information you need. To achieve this, you have to carry out a pilot study or pre-test.</p>
<p>Researchers have given various definitions to these two terms.</p>
<p>A pilot study may be defined as 'a small version of a proposed study conducted to refine the methodology.' It is developed and conducted in a manner similar to the proposed study, using similar research respondents and the same setting. A pilot study may also be defined as 'the process of carrying out a preliminary study going through the entire research procedure with a small sample'.</p>
<p>A pre-test usually refers to a small scale trial of a particular research component. The above definitions illustrate why the two terms are used interchangeably.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260117-34605407.jpeg" alt="img-10.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-research-process">The Research Process<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-research-process" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="why-do-you-need-to-carry-out-a-pre-test">Why do you need to carry out a pre-test?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#why-do-you-need-to-carry-out-a-pre-test" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The following are some of the purposes of a pre-test or pilot study:</p>
<ul>
<li>To determine whether the proposed study is feasible</li>
<li>Identify any problems with the research design</li>
<li>To ensure that items in the data collection instrument are stated clearly and have the same meaning to all research respondents</li>
<li>To assess the time taken to administer the research instrument</li>
<li>Determine whether the sample is representative of the population</li>
<li>To determine the effectiveness of the sampling technique used</li>
<li>Give the researcher the real experience in the field</li>
<li>Determine the human and financial resources requirement for the study</li>
<li>Determine the effectiveness of the training given to research assistants where necessary</li>
<li>Evaluate the procedure for data processing and analysis</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="when-is-the-appropriate-time-to-conduct-a-pre-test">When is the appropriate time to conduct a pre-test?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#when-is-the-appropriate-time-to-conduct-a-pre-test" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Ideally, a pre-test of data collection and data analysis procedures should be made before the actual full scale study. The advantages of conducting the pre-test before you finalise your proposal is that you can draft the work plan and budget based on realistic estimates, as well as revise the data collection tools before you submit your proposal for approval. The pre-test of the data collection instruments could be done either before training your research assistants or immediately after.</p>
<p>However, if this is not possible, for example, if the proposal is drafted far from the field, and there are no similar research settings available close to the drafting site, the field test may be done after finishing the proposal. However, it should be carried out long enough before the actual field work to allow for a thorough revision of data collection tools and procedures.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="using-the-pre-test-findings">Using the Pre-Test Findings<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#using-the-pre-test-findings" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It is important that you, as a researcher, utilise appropriately the lessons learnt from the pre-test or pilot study. For instance:</p>
<ul>
<li>Identify any sensitive or irrelevant items in the instruments and rectify them.</li>
<li>If there was any question that the interviewers had to repeat and explain</li>
</ul>
<p>Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>several times for the respondents, this needs to be rectified.</p>
<ul>
<li>Analyse the results of the pre-test or pilot study as it helps to develop dummy tables.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="ethical-considerations-in-research">Ethical Considerations in Research<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ethical-considerations-in-research" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>As you may have noted in the research process diagram, there is no specific step for 'Ethical Issues in Research'. This is because ethical issues cut across the whole research process.</p>
<p>Ethical considerations start right from the beginning when you are identifying your research problem and continue all the way to the dissemination of the research findings. However, for convenience and academic purposes, you will now look at ethical issues in research at this point</p>
<p>Mugenda and Mugenda (2003:190) describe ethics 'as that branch of philosophy which deals with one's conduct and serves as a guide to one's behaviour'. It is important to note that most professions, including nursing, have a code of conduct to which all members of the profession have to adhere. Similarly, Plooy (2003:118) describes ethics in research as 'that which is morally justifiable'. Research ethics fundamentally consists of collecting, analysing and interpreting data in a way that respects the rights of your participants and respondents.</p>
<p>According to Brink (1996: 39), there are three basic ethical principles that guide researchers including nurse researchers. These are:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="principle-of-respect-for-persons">Principle of Respect for Persons<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#principle-of-respect-for-persons" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This principle involves two main convictions:</p>
<ul>
<li>Individuals are autonomous, that is, they have the right to self-determination and this right should be respected. This means the research respondents have the right to:</li>
</ul>
<p>a) Accept or decline to participate in the study without punishment or prejudice
b) Withdraw from the study at any stage
c) Withhold information
d) Seek clarification concerning the purpose of the study</p>
<ul>
<li>Individuals with diminished autonomy require protection. This group includes children, the mentally impaired, unconscious patients and institutionalised persons. For this group of persons, you will need to seek the consent of their legal guardian.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="principle-of-justice">Principle of Justice<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#principle-of-justice" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This includes the subjects' right to fair selection and treatment and their right to privacy:</p>
<ul>
<li>Right to Privacy: This is the freedom of an individual to determine the time, extent and the circumstances under which private information will be shared with or withheld from others. The privacy of the subject is considered to be protected if the subject is informed and consents to participate in a study and voluntarily shares private information with a researcher. An invasion of privacy occurs when private information is shared without the respondent's knowledge or against their will.</li>
<li>Right to Anonymity and Confidentiality: The research respondents have the right to anonymity and the right to assume that the data collected shall be kept confidential. Complete anonymity exists when the respondents' identity is not revealed and the information collected is not linked to the respondent. Confidentiality refers to the researcher's responsibility to protect all data gathered within the scope of the project from being divulged or made available to any other person, which means the research data should never be shared with outsiders. A breach of confidentiality can occur when a researcher allows an unauthorised person(s) to gain access to the raw data of a study or when the researcher accidentally reveals the identity of the research respondents.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="principle-of-beneficence">Principle of Beneficence<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#principle-of-beneficence" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This principle involves an effort to secure the well being of persons. It is the right to protect respondents from discomfort and harm. This principle states that one should do what is good and above all should do no harm. Discomfort and harm can be physical, emotional, spiritual, economic, social or legal.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-major-content-of-an-informed-consent">The Major Content of an Informed Consent<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-major-content-of-an-informed-consent" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Patients undergoing any operation are required to give informed consent. You may need to obtain informed consent from research respondents who take part in your research project. According Brink (1996:42), informed consent revolves around the following three major elements:</p>
<ul>
<li>The type of information you need to obtain from the research subjects.</li>
</ul>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<ul>
<li>The degree of understanding required of the subject in order to give consent.</li>
<li>The fact that the subject has a free choice in giving consent.</li>
</ul>
<p>Mugenda and Mugenda (2003:192) state that informed consent should be based on the following factors:</p>
<ul>
<li>The purpose of the research study</li>
<li>Foreseen risks</li>
<li>A guarantee of anonymity and confidentiality</li>
<li>Identification of the researcher</li>
<li>Number of subjects involved</li>
<li>Benefits and compensation or lack of them</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="step-eight-data-collection-and-presentation">Step Eight: Data Collection and Presentation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#step-eight-data-collection-and-presentation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Data collection is one of the major steps of the research process. It is the data collection process that will determine the findings of the entire research study. It, therefore, calls for more attention and consideration. There are various data collection methods. However, the approach will depend on the type of study you are undertaking, the study objectives, the design, and above all, the available resources for the study in the form of money, time and the technical skills of the research team.</p>
<p>Burns and Grove (1999: 43) define data collection as 'the precise, systematic gathering of information relevant to the research purpose or the specific objectives, questions or hypotheses of a study'.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-research-process">The Research Process<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-research-process" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260117-dafecbb0.jpeg" alt="img-11.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="data-collection-plan">Data Collection Plan<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#data-collection-plan" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Before you begin the data collection process, you need to draw up a data collection plan. A data collection can be developed using the following steps:</p>
<ul>
<li>List the tasks that have to be carried out and the people responsible for those tasks.</li>
<li>Make a rough estimate of the time needed for different parts of the study.</li>
<li>Identify the most appropriate period in which to carry out the research.</li>
<li>Schedule the different activities that have to be carried out each week in a work plan.</li>
</ul>
<p>A plan for data collection should be developed in order to:</p>
<ul>
<li>Have a clear overview of what tasks have to be carried out, who should perform them and the duration of these tasks.</li>
<li>Organise both human and material resources for data collection in the most efficient way and minimise errors and delays that may result from lack of planning.</li>
<li>Identify problems, for example, limited manpower, that will require modifications to the proposal. Such modification might include adjustment of the sample size or extension of the data collection period.</li>
</ul>
<p>There are three main stages in the data collection process:</p>
<ul>
<li>Stage One - Permission to Proceed</li>
<li>Stage Two - Data Collection</li>
<li>Stage Three - Data Handling</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="stage-one-permission-to-proceed">Stage One: Permission to Proceed<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#stage-one-permission-to-proceed" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Consent must be obtained from the relevant authorities, individuals, and the community in which the project is to be carried out. This may involve organising meetings at national, provincial, district or village level. For clinical studies, this may involve obtaining written informed consent.</p>
<p>The principal investigator is likely to be the one responsible for obtaining permission to proceed at the various levels. The health research unit in the Ministry of Health or the institution organising the research may assist in obtaining permission from the national level. In many countries, research proposals have to be screened for scientific and</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>ethical integrity by national research control centres.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="stage-two-data-collection">Stage Two: Data Collection<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#stage-two-data-collection" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>When collecting data, you have to consider the logistics.</p>
<p>This entails identifying who will collect what, when and with what resources and quality control.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="who-will-collect-what-data">Who will collect what data?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#who-will-collect-what-data" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>When allocating tasks for data collection, it is recommended that you first list the tasks. You can then identify who could best implement each of the tasks. If it is clear beforehand that your research team will not be able to carry out the entire study by itself, you may look for research assistants to assist in relatively simple but time consuming tasks.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Allocating Tasks for Data Collection</th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Task</td><td>To be carried out by</td></tr><tr><td>Record study</td><td>Research team</td></tr><tr><td>Focus group discussion with health staff before and after individual staff interviews</td><td>Research team</td></tr><tr><td>Individual health staff interviews</td><td>Research team</td></tr><tr><td>Participant observation in clinic</td><td>Principal investigator</td></tr><tr><td>Interview with mothers (community based) before and after delivery</td><td>Research assistants, under supervision of research team</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="how-long-does-it-take-to-collect-the-data-for-each-component-of-the-study">How long does it take to collect the data for each component of the study?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#how-long-does-it-take-to-collect-the-data-for-each-component-of-the-study" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In order to answer this question, you should consider the following points:</p>
<ul>
<li>The time required to reach the study area(s).</li>
<li>The time required to 'walk around', and build rapport in the community.</li>
<li>The time required to locate the study units (persons, groups, and records). If you have to search for specific informants, for example, users or defaulters of a specific service, it might take more time to locate informants than to interview them.</li>
<li>The number of visits required per study unit. For some studies, it may be necessary to visit informants a number</li>
</ul>
<p>of times, for instance, if the information needed is sensitive and can only be collected after informants are comfortable with the researcher.</p>
<ul>
<li>The time it takes to verify the quality of data collected in order to establish its validity.</li>
<li>The time required for taking field notes on the research process.</li>
<li>The time required for preliminary analysis of the data.</li>
</ul>
<p>The data collection plan can be drawn up as follows:</p>
<ul>
<li>Calculate the number of interviews that can be carried out each day, for example, four per team member.</li>
<li>Calculate the number of days needed to carry out the interviews. For example, if you need to do 200 interviews, your research team of five people can do <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>5</mn><mo>Ã—</mo><mn>4</mn><mo>=</mo><mn>20</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">5 \times 4 = 20</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7278em; vertical-align: -0.0833em;"></span><span class="mord">5</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mbin">Ã—</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">4</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">20</span></span></span></span></span> interviews per day. You will therefore need 10 (200 Ã· 20) days for the interviews.</li>
<li>Calculate the time needed for the other methods used in the study, for instance, five days for familiarisation and rapport building, five days for focus group discussions, three days for participant observation and so on.</li>
<li>Determine how much time the research team can devote to the fieldwork.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="if-the-time-required-according-to-calculation-is-more-than-the-time-available-it-may-be-necessary-to-recruit-assistants-for-certain-components-of-the-study-for-example-for-conducting-interviews">If the time required according to calculation is more than the time available, it may be necessary to recruit assistants for certain components of the study, for example for conducting interviews.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#if-the-time-required-according-to-calculation-is-more-than-the-time-available-it-may-be-necessary-to-recruit-assistants-for-certain-components-of-the-study-for-example-for-conducting-interviews" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Recruiting research assistants for data collection may ease the task of the research team, but it must be remembered that the training and supervision of research assistants requires time. Time decisions have to be carefully determined on the basis of an analysis of the pros and cons of the study and its team members. If none of the team has previous research experience, they might prefer a study that they can carry out themselves without, or with only minimum, assistance.</p>
<p>If research assistants are required, consider to what extent local health workers can be used. They often have the advantage of being acquainted with the local situations. However, they should never be involved in conducting</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>interviews to evaluate the performance of their own health facility or in studies on people's self care practices in their own catchment areas. Local staff from related services, for example, teachers, community development officers or students could also help with data collection. Occasionally, members or community members can collect certain parts of the village health data.</p>
<p>It is advisable to slightly overestimate the period needed for data collection to allow for unforeseen delays. In fact, it is often difficult to implement the fieldwork strictly according to plan, as you may encounter unforeseen problems, for example, adverse weather conditions or transport breakdown. Your philosophy of fieldwork planning should be flexible.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="in-what-sequence-should-data-be-collected">In what sequence should data be collected?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#in-what-sequence-should-data-be-collected" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Generally, it is advisable to start with available data. This is essential if the sample of respondents is to be selected from records. Another rule of thumb is that qualitative research techniques, for example, focus group discussions, which are devised to focus the content of questionnaires, should be carried out before the finalisation of the questionnaire. If the focus group discussions are to provide feedback on issues raised in larger surveys, however, they should be conducted after preliminary analysis of the questionnaire.</p>
<p>To use time and transport efficiently, data that is to be collected from different sources in one locality should be collected at the same time, for example, interviews with staff in a health centre, observation of the equipment in the centre, and interviews with mothers living nearby should be scheduled together.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="when-should-the-data-be-collected">When should the data be collected?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#when-should-the-data-be-collected" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The type of data to be collected and the demands of the project will determine the actual period of data collection. The following should be considered:</p>
<ul>
<li>The availability of research team members and research assistants.</li>
<li>The appropriate season(s) to conduct the fieldwork (if the problem is season related or if data collection will be difficult in certain periods).</li>
<li>The accessibility and availability of the sample population.</li>
<li>Public holidays and vacation periods.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="ensuring-quality">Ensuring Quality<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ensuring-quality" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It is important that the data collected is of good quality, that is, reliable and valid. Otherwise, you may come out with false or misleading conclusions. Therefore, the possible sources of data distortion (bias) should be addressed. Biases you should try to prevent include deviations from the sampling procedures set out in the proposal, variability and bias in observations or measurements made because:</p>
<ul>
<li>The subject being studied changes their behaviour as a consequence of the research. For example, a subject may act more positively while being observed.</li>
<li>The researcher may use non-standard measuring scales, imprecise or no guidelines for interviewing.</li>
<li>Researchers themselves vary in what they observe or measure, that is, observer variability. For example, researchers may be selective in their observations, that is, observer bias and measure, question, or note down answers with varying accuracy or follow different interview approaches, with one researcher being more open, friendly and probing than the other.</li>
</ul>
<p>As a researcher you should avoid variations in both the measurement criteria and the criteria for categorising answers especially in the course of conducting research. Any changes should have been dealt with prior to conducting the research.</p>
<p>There are a number of measures to be taken to prevent or partly correct such distortions, but remember prevention is better than cure. Cure implies surgery. You may have to cut down parts of your data or at best, devise crutches.</p>
<p>There are several ways of ensuring data quality. Prepare a fieldwork manual for the research team as a whole. The manual should include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Guidelines on sampling procedures and what to do if respondents are not available or refuse to cooperate.</li>
<li>A clear explanation of the purpose and procedures of the study. This should be used as an introduction before each interview.</li>
<li>Instruction sheets on how to ask certain questions and how to record the answers.</li>
</ul>
<p>Ensure that you select your research assistants, if required, with care. Choose assistants who are of the same educational level, familiar with the topic and the local conditions, but are not the</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>subject of study themselves and are not biased concerning the topic.</p>
<p>You should train research assistants carefully in all topics covered in the fieldwork manual as well as in interview techniques. Make sure all members of the research team master techniques such as:</p>
<ul>
<li>Asking questions in a neutral manner.</li>
<li>Not showing, by words or expressions, what answers one expects to hear.</li>
<li>Not showing agreement, disagreement or surprise.</li>
<li>Recording answers precisely as they are provided, without sifting through them or interpreting them.</li>
</ul>
<p>You should also pre-test research instruments and research procedures with the whole research team, including research assistants. Take care that research assistants are not placed under too much stress, for example, too many interviews a day.</p>
<p>Arrange for ongoing supervision of research assistants. If you are carrying out a substantially large survey, special supervisors may have to be appointed with supervisory guidelines.</p>
<p>It is also important to devise methods to ensure that the data collected by all members of the research team is of quality. You can do this by:</p>
<ul>
<li>Having interviewers check whether the questionnaire is filled in completely before finishing each interview.</li>
<li>Asking the supervisors to check at the end of each day during the data collection period whether the questionnaires are filled in completely and whether the recorded information makes sense.</li>
<li>Having the researchers review the data during the data analysis stage to check whether data is complete and consistent.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="stage-three-data-handling">Stage Three: Data Handling<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#stage-three-data-handling" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Once the data has been collected, a clear procedure should be developed for handling and storing it. The following should act as guidelines:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Check that the data gathered is complete and accurate.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>At some stage questionnaires will have to be numbered. Decide if this should be done at the time of the interview or when the questionnaires are stored.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Identify the person responsible for storing data and the place where it will be stored.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Decide how data should be stored. Record forms should be kept in the sequence in which they have been numbered.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="data-entry">Data Entry<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#data-entry" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There are several factors you need to consider when entering your data. First, you will need to decide on a format, that is, the way you will organise the data in a file. Next, you will need to design a code, that is, the rules by which the respondents' answers will be assigned values that can be processed by the computer.</p>
<p>After this you then do the actual coding, that is, turn the responses into the standard categories you developed in your coding system. Data entry is the next step, which is keying the data into the computer so that you can process it. Finally, data cleaning is the final check you make on the data file for accuracy, completeness, and consistency prior to the onset of analysis.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="you-should-always-ensure-that-your-data-collection-process-is-ethical-in-all-respects">You should always ensure that your data collection process is ethical in all respects.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#you-should-always-ensure-that-your-data-collection-process-is-ethical-in-all-respects" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In order to ensure that your data collection process is ethical, you should consider the following points:</p>
<ul>
<li>How have you planned to obtain informed consent from your informants?</li>
<li>Are there any categories of informants that need special consideration because of their vulnerable status, for example, children, sick persons, and mentally disabled individuals?</li>
<li>Are certain parts of the research focussed on sensitive issues?</li>
<li>How will you handle problems that may arise?</li>
<li>Do certain parts of your research require extra attention to assure confidentiality?</li>
<li>How will you handle this issue?</li>
</ul>
<p>Once you have answered all these questions you are well on your way to ensuring that high ethical standards are maintained throughout the course of your research project.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="training-of-interviewers">Training of Interviewers<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#training-of-interviewers" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>During the fieldwork, interviews or research assistants may work independently or with one</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>of the researchers. If research assistants are to go out independently, they may have to carry out the following tasks:</p>
<ul>
<li>Do sampling in the field, for example, sampling households.</li>
<li>Give clear introductions to the interviewee concerning the purpose and procedures of the interview.</li>
<li>Perform the interview. It is important to give interviewers a standard questionnaire to administer. However, it is not wise to assign difficult tasks, like Focus Group Discussions (FGDs), to research assistants.</li>
</ul>
<p>It is, therefore, imperative that you properly train interviewers so that they can carry out their tasks accurately and correctly, according to the procedures you have developed. They should never be left to develop their own procedures as this could potentially lead to bias. The training of interviewers may take 2-3 days. The first day should be devoted to theory, followed by one or two days of practical training, depending on the local circumstances and the nature of the study.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="theoretical-training">Theoretical Training<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#theoretical-training" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Research assistants or interviewers must be thoroughly familiar with the research objectives and the methodology. They should, therefore, be provided with a copy of the research protocol. You, as the principle researcher, should discuss important sections of the protocol with them.</p>
<p>â˜ Statement of the problem
â˜ Objectives
â˜ Data collection tools to be used
â˜ Sampling procedure
â˜ Plan for data collection
â˜ Plan for data analysis</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="step-eight-data-collection-and-presentation-practical-training">Step Eight: Data Collection and Presentation Practical Training<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#step-eight-data-collection-and-presentation-practical-training" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Practical training in interviewing is very important. This may be provided in two stages. First, a role play can be performed during which one of the trainees assumes the role of an interviewer and the other plays the interviewee. Second, a field test should be conducted which will serve two purposes: the training of interviewers and as a test of the data collection tools.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="supervision-of-interviewers">Supervision of Interviewers<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#supervision-of-interviewers" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>If interviewers are used, the responsibility for the research remains with the</p>
<p>principle researcher. To guarantee the quality of the data collected:</p>
<ul>
<li>It is important to supervise the performance of the interviewers, especially at the beginning of the data collection period.</li>
<li>If they are to go out into the field independently, plans should be made to visit them on different occasions.</li>
<li>As a further quality check, it is important that each questionnaire carries the name or initials of the interviewer, so that it is possible to ask for clarification if certain information is not clear.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="step-nine-data-analysis-and-interpretation">Step Nine: Data Analysis and Interpretation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#step-nine-data-analysis-and-interpretation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In step eight you learnt methods of data collection. Once you have collected the data according to the planned research design, it is important to put the data in a format that will enable you to make sense of it. In step nine you will learn how to present, analyse and interpret the data collected. You will also need to refer to module three, unit five on Community Diagnosis for</p>
<p>further clarification.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="data-presentation">Data Presentation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#data-presentation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Data presentation is the in which data is displayed for viewing, interpreting and understanding. In section two of this unit, you covered the major types of research, which you broadly classified as qualitative and quantitative. You also identified the specific characteristics of each type. In this part of the research process, you will look at how data from either type are presented and analysed.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260117-cfacd7c8.jpeg" alt="img-12.jpeg">
The Research Process</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 106 of 185
Bishop's Computers</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="methods-of-data-presentation">Methods of Data Presentation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#methods-of-data-presentation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>There are several ways in which data can be presented.</p>
<p>The following are some methods of data presentation, which you may use:</p>
<ul>
<li>Tables</li>
<li>Charts</li>
<li>Graphs</li>
<li>Frequency distribution tables</li>
<li>Histograms</li>
<li>Narrative method</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="qualitative-data-presentation-and-analysis">Qualitative Data Presentation and Analysis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#qualitative-data-presentation-and-analysis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The data presentation and analysis of qualitative research is quite different from that of presenting data collected when using the quantitative research method. This is because qualitative research uses words while quantitative uses numbers (numerical).</p>
<p>However, the principles are the same for both types. In both cases the researcher has to do the following:</p>
<p>a) Describe the sample population by providing a description of the:</p>
<ul>
<li>Respondents, for instance, key informants or focus group members.</li>
<li>Age, sex, occupation, educational background and so on.</li>
</ul>
<p>b) Order, reduce and/or code the data (data processing).</p>
<p>c) Display the summaries of data for interpretation.</p>
<p>d) Draw conclusions.</p>
<p>e) Develop strategies for testing or confirming the findings to prove their validity.</p>
<p>The following steps can be used when presenting and analysing qualitative data:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="organising-the-data">Organising the Data<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#organising-the-data" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The data collected through qualitative techniques is usually quite large, therefore, the researcher will need to put all this information in a simple format that can be understood. This is known as 'cleaning' the data.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="creating-categories-themes-and-patterns">Creating Categories, Themes and Patterns<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#creating-categories-themes-and-patterns" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Since the notes from interviews and focus group discussions are not well organised and tend to be fragmented, there is need to put them into categories, themes and patterns. The researcher needs to be very familiar with the data so as to establish relationships among these categories. One way to achieve this is using the research questions or discussion topics. The researcher may need to put this into matrix form on which the data can be displayed.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="analysing-and-interpreting-the-data">Analysing and Interpreting the Data<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#analysing-and-interpreting-the-data" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Once themes, categories and patterns have been identified, the researcher then evaluates the data to determine its usefulness and accuracy. It is important to note that there are nowadays computer programs that can be used to generate themes and categories. You have to follow the set research objectives when analysing and evaluating the data.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="writing-the-report">Writing the Report<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#writing-the-report" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>It is important to appreciate that unlike the quantitative research where the report writing is done after analysing the data, in qualitative research techniques, the writing and the analysis go hand in hand.</p>
<p>So far you have seen how qualitative data is analysed and presented in a research report. You will now look at how to analyse and present quantitative data. You will note that here the raw data you collect will usually be in the form of numbers. The process of organising, summarising and visualising quantitative data is referred to as 'descriptive statistics' (Eden, 2003: 211).</p>
<p>The purpose of descriptive statistics is to help the researcher to visualise and identify the patterns that may emerge from the data collected. This will, in turn, assist the researcher in making meaningful conclusions from the results of the study. The main types of descriptive statistics are mode, median, mean and measures of central tendency among others. Refer to module three where these types have already been covered.</p>
<p>In presenting quantitative data, it is very important that you use what is referred to as dummy tables. A dummy table contains all the characteristics of a table but the cells are left empty. Dummy tables are usually constructed during the planning stage of data collection. This helps the researcher visualise how to organise the data and put it in a summarised format. Dummy tables can be used for depicting simple data as well as complex data that could be used for cross tabulation.</p>
<p>In presenting data you can use some of the following methods detailed on the next page in order to make sense of what the results mean.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="tabular-method">Tabular Method<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tabular-method" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>As the name suggests, this is where data is presented in tables. Depending on your skills in research and the type of study you have carried out, there are various tabular methods that you could use.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="simple-table">Simple Table<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#simple-table" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is usually a single line of characters explaining a few columns of information.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="tabular-method">Tabular Method<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tabular-method" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Mothers Attending Antenatal Clinic</th><th></th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Age of Mothers in Years</td><td>Numbers of Mothers (n=100)</td><td>Percentage</td></tr><tr><td>19-24</td><td>35</td><td>35%</td></tr><tr><td>25-28</td><td>20</td><td>20%</td></tr><tr><td>29-33</td><td>16</td><td>16%</td></tr><tr><td>34-38</td><td>05</td><td>5%</td></tr><tr><td>39 and above</td><td>-</td><td>-</td></tr><tr><td>Total</td><td>76</td><td>76%</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="compound-table">Compound Table<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#compound-table" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is where a single line of characters has been described by two or more components of information.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="tabular-methods">Tabular Methods<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tabular-methods" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Face-to-Face Class Attendance of the Distance Learning Programme</th><th></th><th></th><th></th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Months</td><td>Public Hospital (n=100)</td><td>Private Hospital (n=100)</td><td>Mission Hospital (n=100)</td><td>Total</td></tr><tr><td>January</td><td>100</td><td>90</td><td>100</td><td>290</td></tr><tr><td>February</td><td>89</td><td>95</td><td>85</td><td>269</td></tr><tr><td>March</td><td>95</td><td>100</td><td>80</td><td>275</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>When constructing tables it is important that:</p>
<ul>
<li>You give correct consecutive numbers to the tables and indicate the title of the table at the top.</li>
<li>Clearly indicate the totals and percentages.</li>
<li>Show where the data is obtained from, that is the source of the data with the year when the data was collected clearly shown.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="graphic-method">Graphic Method<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#graphic-method" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Another format for presenting research data is the graphic method. In this method graphs are used to organise and describe data. This enables the reader to see at a glance the trend of distribution of the data. Graphs have two axes: vertical and horizontal. Scores are usually presented along the horizontal axis, while the frequency is placed along the vertical axis. It is important to note that the intersection point between the vertical and horizontal axes is usually represented by a zero (0). Graphs should be well labelled both along the vertical axis and the horizontal. There are three common graphic methods that you may use for presenting your data.</p>
<p>These are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Bar Charts</li>
<li>Histograms</li>
<li>Frequency Polygons</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="bar-charts">Bar Charts<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#bar-charts" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Bar charts are used when the data being presented is discrete or when the scale is nominal. Bar charts are quite similar to histograms with the exception that there are spaces in bar charts.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260117-f06fb827.jpeg" alt="img-13.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="histograms">Histograms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#histograms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Unlike the bar chart, in the histogram there are no spaces between the bars. A histogram in many cases is used to represent continuous variables.</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260117-e2287ec8.jpeg" alt="img-14.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="frequency-polygons">Frequency Polygons<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#frequency-polygons" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Frequency polygons are drawn based on the frequencies of the observations along the vertical axis against the group or class midpoints.</p>
<p>They form a polygon shape hence the name.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260117-21978088.jpeg" alt="img-15.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="charts-method">Charts Method<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#charts-method" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Another way to present your data is to place it on a chart. One of the commonest types of charts used is the pie chart. Pie charts are relatively easy to interpret. Each portion represents a variable. The adjacent figure shows the top four outpatient cases in a hospital. Note that, in order to present your data on a pie chart, you have to convert it into percentages.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260117-afe82b25.jpeg" alt="img-16.jpeg">
Pie Chart Percentage Causes of Maternal Mortality</p>
